	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Scholasticism</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Predominant method of critical thought in academic pedagogy of medieval European universities, circa 1100–1700</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Scholastics" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Scholastic_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Scholastic (disambiguation)">Scholastic</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Scholarism" title="Scholarism">Scholarism</a>.</div>

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<p><a href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="046CupolaSPietro.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" decoding="async" width="16" height="12" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism&#32;portal</a>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png" class="image"><img alt="Socrates.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" data-file-width="326" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Scholasticism</b> was a <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> school of philosophy that employed a critical method of philosophical analysis presupposed upon a <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin</a> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Theistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Theistic">theistic</a> paradigm which dominated teaching in the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">medieval universities</a> in Europe from about 1100 to 1700. It originated within the Christian <a href="/wiki/Monastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monastic">monastic</a> schools that were the basis of the earliest European universities.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The rise of scholasticism was closely associated with the rise of the 12th and 13th century schools that developed into the earliest modern universities, including those in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholasticism is not so much a philosophy or a theology as a method of learning, as it places a strong emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_reasoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Dialectical reasoning">dialectical reasoning</a> to extend knowledge by <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a> and to resolve <a href="/wiki/Contradictions" class="mw-redirect" title="Contradictions">contradictions</a>. Scholastic thought is also known for rigorous conceptual analysis and the careful drawing of distinctions. In the classroom and in writing, it often takes the form of explicit <a href="/wiki/Disputation" title="Disputation">disputation</a>; a topic drawn from the tradition is broached in the form of a question, opponents' responses are given, a counterproposal is argued and opponents' arguments rebutted. Because of its emphasis on rigorous dialectical method, scholasticism was eventually applied to many other fields of study.<sup id="cite_ref-cambridgedict_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cambridgedict-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a program, scholasticism began as an attempt at harmonization on the part of medieval Christian thinkers, to harmonize the various authorities of their own tradition, and to reconcile Christian theology with classical and late antiquity philosophy, especially that of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> but also of <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">Christian apologetics</a>.)
</p><p>Some of the main figures of scholasticism include <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a> (the "father of scholasticism"<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Peter_Abelard" title="Peter Abelard">Peter Abelard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Hales" title="Alexander of Hales">Alexander of Hales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duns_Scotus" title="Duns Scotus">Duns Scotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure" title="Bonaventure">Bonaventure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>. Aquinas's masterwork <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i> (1265–1274) is considered to be the pinnacle of scholastic, medieval, and Christian philosophy;<sup id="cite_ref-GilsonP_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GilsonP-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> it began while Aquinas was regent master at the <i>studium provinciale</i> of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Sabina" title="Santa Sabina">Santa Sabina</a> in Rome, the forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas, <i>Angelicum</i></a>. Important work in the scholastic tradition has been carried on well past Aquinas's time, for instance by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Suárez">Francisco Suárez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Molina" title="Luis de Molina">Luis de Molina</a>, and also among Lutheran and Reformed thinkers. The historical legacy of scholasticism lay not in specific scientific discoveries, for these were not made,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> but laying the foundations for the development of natural science.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The terms "scholastic" and "scholasticism" derive from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">scholasticus</i></i>, the Latinized form of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">σχολαστικός</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">scholastikos</i></i>), an adjective derived from <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">σχολή</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">scholē</i></i>), "<a href="/wiki/School" title="School">school</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <i>Scholasticus</i> means "of or pertaining to schools". The "scholastics" were, roughly, "schoolmen".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The foundations of Christian scholasticism were laid by <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a> through his logical and theological essays,<sup id="cite_ref-cambridgedict_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cambridgedict-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and later forerunners (and then companions) to scholasticism were Islamic <a href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81m" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalām">Ilm al-Kalām</a>, literally "science of discourse",<sup id="cite_ref-Wolfson_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wolfson-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy#Jewish_scholarship_after_destruction_of_Second_Temple" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish philosophy</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Kalam" title="Jewish Kalam">Jewish Kalam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Scholasticism">Early Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first significant renewal of learning in the West came with the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, advised by <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Pisa" title="Peter of Pisa">Peter of Pisa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcuin_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcuin of York">Alcuin of York</a>, attracted the scholars of England and Ireland. By decree in AD 787, he established schools in every abbey in his empire.  These schools, from which the name <i>scholasticism</i> is derived, became centers of medieval learning.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During this period, knowledge of Ancient Greek had vanished in the West except in Ireland, where its teaching and use was widely dispersed in the <a href="/wiki/Monastic_school" title="Monastic school">monastic schools</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The citation is not specific enough to facilitate source verification (November 2018)">not specific enough to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Irish scholars had a considerable presence in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Frankish court</a>, where they were renowned for their learning.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Among them was <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Scotus_Eriugena" class="mw-redirect" title="Johannes Scotus Eriugena">Johannes Scotus Eriugena</a> (815–877), one of the founders of scholasticism.<sup id="cite_ref-Gothic_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gothic-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Eriugena was the most significant Irish intellectual of the early monastic period and an outstanding philosopher in terms of originality.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He had considerable familiarity with the Greek language and translated many works into Latin, affording access to the <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Fathers" title="Cappadocian Fathers">Cappadocian Fathers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Christian_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox Christian theology">Greek theological tradition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StanfordEriugena_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordEriugena-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The other three founders of scholasticism were the 11th-century scholars <a href="/wiki/Peter_Abelard" title="Peter Abelard">Peter Abelard</a>, Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Lanfranc_of_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Lanfranc of Canterbury">Lanfranc of Canterbury</a> and Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gothic_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gothic-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This period saw the beginning of the '<a href="/wiki/Transmission_of_Greek_philosophical_ideas_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Transmission of Greek philosophical ideas in the Middle Ages">rediscovery</a>' of many Greek works which had been lost to the Latin West.  As early as the 10th century, scholars in Spain had begun to gather translated texts and, in the latter half of that century, began transmitting them to the rest of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindberg197860–61_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindberg197860–61-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> After a successful burst of <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> in the 12th century, Spain opened even further for Christian scholars, and as these Europeans encountered Islamic philosophy, they opened a wealth of Arab knowledge of mathematics and astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-institutions_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-institutions-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Adelard_of_Bath" title="Adelard of Bath">Adelard of Bath</a> traveled to Spain and Sicily, translating works on astronomy and mathematics, including the first complete translation of <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements">Elements</a></i> into Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClagett1982356_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClagett1982356-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the same time, <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Laon" title="Anselm of Laon">Anselm of Laon</a> systematized the production of the <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">gloss</a> on Scripture, followed by the rise to prominence of <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a> (the middle subject of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Trivium_(education)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trivium (education)">trivium</a>) in the work of <a href="/wiki/Abelard" class="mw-redirect" title="Abelard">Abelard</a>. <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a> produced a collection of <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a>,</i> or opinions of the Church Fathers and other authorities<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Scholasticism">High Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: High Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 13th and early 14th centuries are generally seen as the high period of scholasticism. The early 13th century witnessed the culmination of the <a href="/wiki/Recovery_of_Aristotle" title="Recovery of Aristotle">recovery of Greek philosophy</a>. Schools of translation grew up in Italy and Sicily, and eventually in the rest of Europe.  Powerful Norman kings gathered men of knowledge from Italy and other areas into their courts as a sign of their prestige.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELindberg197870–72_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELindberg197870–72-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_of_Moerbeke" title="William of Moerbeke">William of Moerbeke</a>'s translations and editions of Greek philosophical texts in the middle half of the thirteenth century helped form a clearer picture of Greek philosophy, particularly of Aristotle, than was given by the Arabic versions on which they had previously relied. <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grant" title="Edward Grant">Edward Grant</a> writes "Not only was the structure of the Arabic language radically different from that of Latin, but some Arabic versions had been derived from earlier Syriac translations and were thus twice removed from the original Greek text. Word-for-word translations of such Arabic texts could produce tortured readings. By contrast, the structural closeness of Latin to Greek, permitted literal, but intelligible, word-for-word translations."<sup id="cite_ref-institutions_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-institutions-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/University" title="University">Universities</a> developed in the large cities of Europe during this period, and rival clerical orders within the church began to battle for political and intellectual control over these centers of educational life. The two main orders founded in this period were the <a href="/wiki/Franciscans" title="Franciscans">Franciscans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a>. The Franciscans were founded by <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">Francis of Assisi</a> in 1209. Their leader in the middle of the century was <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure" title="Bonaventure">Bonaventure</a>, a traditionalist who defended the theology of <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a> and the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, incorporating only a little of Aristotle in with the more neoplatonist elements. Following Anselm, Bonaventure supposed that reason can only discover truth when philosophy is illuminated by religious faith.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Other important Franciscan scholastics were <a href="/wiki/Duns_Scotus" title="Duns Scotus">Duns Scotus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Auriol" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Auriol">Peter Auriol</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, the Dominican order, a teaching order founded by <a href="/wiki/St_Dominic" class="mw-redirect" title="St Dominic">St Dominic</a> in 1215, to propagate and defend Christian doctrine, placed more emphasis on the use of reason and made extensive use of the <a href="/wiki/Recovery_of_Aristotle" title="Recovery of Aristotle">new Aristotelian sources</a> derived from the East and Moorish Spain. The great representatives of Dominican thinking in this period were <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> and (especially) <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, whose artful synthesis of Greek rationalism and Christian doctrine eventually came to define Catholic philosophy. Aquinas placed more emphasis on reason and argumentation, and was one of the first to use the new translation of Aristotle's metaphysical and epistemological writing. This was a significant departure from the <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonic</a> and Augustinian thinking that had dominated much of early scholasticism. Aquinas showed how it was possible to incorporate much of the philosophy of Aristotle without falling into the errors of the Commentator, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Scholasticism">Spanish Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spanish Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Scholasticism">Late Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Late Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_scholasticism" title="Second scholasticism">Second scholasticism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lutheran_Scholasticism">Lutheran Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lutheran Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_scholasticism" title="Lutheran scholasticism">Lutheran scholasticism</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformed_Scholasticism">Reformed Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reformed Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reformed_scholasticism" title="Reformed scholasticism">Reformed scholasticism</a></div>
<p>Following the Reformation, <a href="/wiki/Calvinists" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinists">Calvinists</a> largely adopted the scholastic method of theology, while differing regarding sources of authority and content of theology.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Scholasticism">Neo-Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Neo-Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neo-scholasticism" title="Neo-scholasticism">Neo-scholasticism</a></div>
<p>The revival and development from the second half of the 19th century of medieval scholastic philosophy is sometimes called neo-<a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thomistic_Scholasticism">Thomistic Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Thomistic Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As J. A. Weisheipl <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Preachers" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Preachers">O.P.</a> emphasizes, within the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a> Thomistic scholasticism has been continuous since the time of Aquinas: "Thomism was always alive in the Dominican Order, small as it was after the ravages of the Reformation, the French Revolution, and the Napoleonic occupation. Repeated legislation of the General Chapters, beginning after the death of St. Thomas, as well as the Constitutions of the Order, required all Dominicans to teach the doctrine of St. Thomas both in philosophy and in theology."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomistic scholasticism or scholastic <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a> identifies with the philosophical and theological tradition stretching back to the time of St. Thomas.  It focuses not only on exegesis of the historical Aquinas but also on the articulation of a rigorous system of orthodox Thomism to be used as an instrument of critique of contemporary thought.  Due to its suspicion of attempts to harmonize Aquinas with non-Thomistic categories and assumptions, Scholastic Thomism has sometimes been called, according to philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Edward_Feser" title="Edward Feser">Edward Feser</a>, "Strict Observance Thomism".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  A discussion of recent and current Thomistic scholasticism can be found in <i>La Metafisica di san Tommaso d'Aquino e i suoi interpreti</i> (2002) by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Battista_Mondin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Battista Mondin (page does not exist)">Battista Mondin</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battista_Mondin" class="extiw" title="it:Battista Mondin">it</a>&#93;</span>, which includes such figures as Sofia Vanni Rovighi (1908–1990),<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cornelio_Fabro" title="Cornelio Fabro">Cornelio Fabro</a> (1911–1995), Carlo Giacon (1900–1984),<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tomas_Tyn" class="mw-redirect" title="Tomas Tyn">Tomas Tyn</a> <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Preachers" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Preachers">O.P.</a> (1950–1990), Abelardo Lobato <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Preachers" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Preachers">O.P.</a> (1925–2012), Leo Elders (1926– ) and <a href="/wiki/Ventimiglia_Giovanni" class="mw-redirect" title="Ventimiglia Giovanni">Giovanni Ventimiglia</a> (1964– ) among others.  Fabro in particular emphasizes Aquinas' originality, especially with respect to the <i>actus essendi</i> or act of existence of finite beings by participating in being itself.  Other scholars such as those involved with the "Progetto Tommaso" seek to establish an objective and universal reading of Aquinas' texts.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomistic scholasticism in the English speaking world went into decline in the 1970s when the Thomistic revival that had been spearheaded by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Maritain" title="Jacques Maritain">Jacques Maritain</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Gilson" title="Étienne Gilson">Étienne Gilson</a>, and others, diminished in influence. Partly, this was because this branch of <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomism</a> had become a quest to understand the historical Aquinas after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analytical_Scholasticism">Analytical Scholasticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Analytical Scholasticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A renewed interest in the "scholastic" way of doing philosophy has recently awoken in the confines of the <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a>. Attempts emerged to combine elements of scholastic and analytic methodology in pursuit of a contemporary philosophical synthesis. Proponents of various incarnations of this approach include <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Kenny" title="Anthony Kenny">Anthony Kenny</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_King_(philosopher)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Peter King (philosopher) (page does not exist)">Peter King</a>, Thomas Williams or <a href="/wiki/David_Oderberg" class="mw-redirect" title="David Oderberg">David Oderberg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Analytical_Thomism" title="Analytical Thomism">Analytical Thomism</a> can be seen as a pioneer part of this movement.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="we need done boys (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholastic_method">Scholastic method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Scholastic method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cornelius O'Boyle explained that Scholasticism focuses on how to acquire knowledge and how to communicate effectively so that it may be acquired by others. It was thought that the best way to achieve this was by replicating the discovery process (<i>modus inveniendi</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The scholasticists would choose a book by a renowned scholar, <i><a href="/wiki/Auctor" title="Auctor">auctor</a></i> (author), as a subject for investigation.  By reading it thoroughly and critically, the disciples learned to appreciate the theories of the author.  Other documents related to the book would be referenced, such as Church councils, papal letters and anything else written on the subject, be it ancient or contemporary.  The points of disagreement and contention between multiple sources would be written down in individual sentences or snippets of text, known as <a href="/wiki/Sententiae" class="mw-redirect" title="Sententiae">sententiae</a>. Once the sources and points of disagreement had been laid out through a series of <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectics</a>, the two sides of an argument would be made whole so that they would be found to be in agreement and not contradictory. (Of course, sometimes opinions would be totally rejected, or new positions proposed.) This was done in two ways. The first was through <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philological</a> analysis.  Words were examined and argued to have multiple meanings.  It was also considered that the <i>auctor</i> might have intended a certain word to mean something different.  Ambiguity could be used to find common ground between two otherwise contradictory statements. The second was through logical analysis, which relied on the rules of formal <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> – as they were known at the time – to show that contradictions did not exist but were subjective to the reader.<sup id="cite_ref-westintel_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-westintel-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholastic_instruction">Scholastic instruction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scholastic instruction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Scholastic instruction consisted of several elements. The first was the <i>lectio</i>: a teacher would read an authoritative text followed by a commentary, but no questions were permitted. This was followed by the <i>meditatio</i> (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meditatio" class="extiw" title="wikt:meditatio">meditation</a> or reflection) in which students reflected on and appropriated the text. Finally, in the <i>quaestio</i> students could ask questions (<i>quaestiones</i>) that might have occurred to them during <i>meditatio</i>. Eventually the discussion of <i>questiones</i> became a method of inquiry apart from the <i>lectio</i> and independent of authoritative texts. <i>Disputationes</i> were arranged to resolve controversial <i>quaestiones</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201159_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201159-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Questions to be disputed were ordinarily announced beforehand, but students could propose a question to the teacher unannounced – <i>disputationes de quodlibet</i>. In this case, the teacher responded and the students rebutted; on the following day the teacher, having used notes taken during the disputation, summarised all arguments and presented his final position, riposting all rebuttals.<sup id="cite_ref-westintel_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-westintel-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201160_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201160-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>quaestio</i> method of reasoning was initially used especially when two authoritative texts seemed to contradict one another. Two contradictory propositions would be considered in the form of an either/or question, and each part of the question would have to be approved (<i>sic</i>) or denied (<i>non</i>). Arguments for the position taken would be presented in turn, followed by arguments against the position, and finally the arguments against would be refuted. This method forced scholars to consider opposing viewpoints and defend their own arguments against them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201161–62_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvan_Asselt201161–62-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scholasticism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_scholastic_philosophers" title="List of scholastic philosophers">List of scholastic philosophers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">Medieval philosophy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">Nominalism</a></li>
